<div id="review_1553362888" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1553362888" />
    <a title="Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6354112-perry"><img alt="Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588528919p2/6354112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1553362888?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1553362888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer198134913115222315"><b>
  <u>A Supercollider Story</u>
</b><br/>"In the jungle, the mighty jungle<br/>The lion sleeps tonight.<br/>Wimoweh, wimoweh, wimoweh, wimoweh<br/>***<br/>Near the village, the peaceful village, <br/>The lion sleeps tonight."<br/>The Tokens, <i>The Lion Sleeps Tonight</i>, 1961<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1466974593i/19536917._SX540_.jpg' width='320' height='166' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Named by <i>Time</i> magazine as 1995's Best Fiction, <i>American Tabloid</i> is the literary equivalent of "packing heat and unloading." Written in a pugnacious style I haven't really read before, the book centers on 3 men: Kemper Boyd, a philandering FBI agent recruited by J. Edgar Hoov</span>
  <span id="freeText198134913115222315" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>A Supercollider Story</u>
</b><br>"In the jungle, the mighty jungle<br>The lion sleeps tonight.<br>Wimoweh, wimoweh, wimoweh, wimoweh<br>***<br>Near the village, the peaceful village, <br>The lion sleeps tonight."<br>The Tokens, <i>The Lion Sleeps Tonight</i>, 1961<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1466974593i/19536917._SX540_.jpg" width="320" height="166" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Named by <i>Time</i> magazine as 1995's Best Fiction, <i>American Tabloid</i> is the literary equivalent of "packing heat and unloading." Written in a pugnacious style I haven't really read before, the book centers on 3 men: Kemper Boyd, a philandering FBI agent recruited by J. Edgar Hoover to infiltrate the Kennedy clan via Bobby's efforts to prosecute Jimmy Hoffa and his mob associates; Ward Littell, an obdurate alcoholic FBI agent who's on the outs with Hoover and ultimately becomes connected with organized crime in Chicago and Howard Hughes; and, Pete Bondurant, a bad-to-the-bone heavy lifter, former law enforcement officer, who works for Hughes, Hoffa and ultimately with the CIA. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1466974765i/19536920.jpg" width="300" height="207" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>J. Edgar Hoover (in men's clothes)<br><br>The story takes place over 5 years from November 1958 through the day of Pres. Kennedy's assassination, covering shakedowns, collusions, heroin, the Bay of Pigs, numerous hits and a treasonous "contract." In addition to the H's (Hoover, Hughes and Hoffa), the novel is filled with FBI and CIA officials, anti-Castro rebels, the Hollywood crowd, CIA officials, Cuban commies and various mobsters from New York, Chicago and Miami. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1466974894i/19536921._SX540_.jpg" width="311" height="175" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Howard Hughes<br><br>Ellroy pulls no punches in this explosive novel that dashes from page 1 to its fateful finish.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1466975157i/19536937._SX540_.jpg" width="351" height="231" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Castro with Khrushchev at Kremlin, 1963<br><br>Edit: It takes a few minutes to get acclimated to Ellroy's writing style. Brash, short in-your-face paragraphs. Exhibit 1: <blockquote> “America was never innocent. We popped our cherry on the boat over and looked back with no regrets. You can't ascribe our fall from grace to any single event or set of circumstances. You can't lose what you lacked at conception.<br>***<br>The real Trinity of Camelot was Look Good, Kick Ass, Get Laid. Jack Kennedy was the mythological front man for a particularly juicy slice of our history. He called a slick line and wore a world-class haircut. He was Bill Clinton minus pervasive media scrutiny and a few rolls of flab.<br><br>Jack got whacked at the optimum moment to assure his sainthood. Lies continue to swirl around his eternal flame. It's time to dislodge his urn and cast light on a few men who attended his ascent and facilitated his fall.<br><br>They were rough cops and shakedown artists. ... wiretappers and soldiers of fortune and faggot lounge entertainers. Had one second of their lives deviated off course, American History would not exist as we know it.<br><br>It's time to demythologize an era and build a new myth from the gutter to the stars. It's time to embrace bad men and the price they paid to secretly define their time.<br><br>Here's to them.”</blockquote> </span>
  <a data-text-id="198134913115222315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1063312927" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1063312927" />
    <a title="Ɗẳɳ  2.☊" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12058788-2"><img alt="Ɗẳɳ  2.☊" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589380555p2/12058788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <a title="Ɗẳɳ  2.☊" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ɗẳɳ  2.☊" href="/user/show/12058788-2">Ɗẳɳ  2.☊</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12058788-2?shelf=goldilocks-zone">goldilocks-zone</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer17290470687970574841">Okay, I will reluctantly post my first review here. Up to now, I've never really felt the need to do my own reviews. I'd rather leave that to the semi-professional book bloggers & "power users" out there. Who, quite frankly, are much better at it than me. Typically, once I finish a book, I merely post my take on whichever friends' reviews I happen to like the most. I also find myself skimming many long reviews so I hope to keep this short. I'll leave the synopsis for the pros. So without further</span>
  <span id="freeText17290470687970574841" style="display:none">Okay, I will reluctantly post my first review here. Up to now, I've never really felt the need to do my own reviews. I'd rather leave that to the semi-professional book bloggers &amp; "power users" out there. Who, quite frankly, are much better at it than me. Typically, once I finish a book, I merely post my take on whichever friends' reviews I happen to like the most. I also find myself skimming many long reviews so I hope to keep this short. I'll leave the synopsis for the pros. So without further ado, here's my take:<br /><br />"Wow! This is a tough one to rate. I loved the beginning, but it eventually began to wear me down. The writing style is so jarring, and there is so much story here (it felt like 1000 pages crammed into 600), and some parts just didn't as interesting me. But I was way too invested in the story to throw in the towel, so I cranked up the coffee machine and fought through it. Then the strangest thing happened. When I upped my pace and quit trying to connect every tiny thread the story really took off, and the final third of the book was brilliant.<br /><br />Dig that roller coaster ride. A 4-star whoosh outta the gate, lags to a 2-star middling slog, coming down the homestretch BANG 5 stars with a bullet!" <br /><br />I greedily hoard my 5-star rankings for those rare books which I find little to complain about. Here the ADD writing style, overstuffed narrative, and an abundance of tedious minutiae drop this one down a bit.<br /><br />Thanks to Kemper for leading me to this one: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6595494?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a></span>

<div id="review_9433855" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9433855" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/126644-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1269278346p2/126644.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9433855?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff" href="/user/show/126644-jeff">Jeff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">central north carolina</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9433855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7699532725595794736">while ellroy's chandler-on-crack routine is exhausting stylistically [mock sample excerpt: "this spic commie was a real cooze hound. dig his geeked-out arsenal: 20 30.06 shells, three silencer-rigged .45s, a rapemobile-mounted shotgun. agency/outfit sanctioned figured kemper boyd."], _american tabloid_'s dark reimagining of early-60s optimism as a cesspool of cynical political power plays underscored by mixed alliances, double- and triple-crosses, and the reduction of the era's most "powerful/in</span>
  <span id="freeText7699532725595794736" style="display:none">while ellroy's chandler-on-crack routine is exhausting stylistically [mock sample excerpt: "this spic commie was a real cooze hound. dig his geeked-out arsenal: 20 30.06 shells, three silencer-rigged .45s, a rapemobile-mounted shotgun. agency/outfit sanctioned figured kemper boyd."], _american tabloid_'s dark reimagining of early-60s optimism as a cesspool of cynical political power plays underscored by mixed alliances, double- and triple-crosses, and the reduction of the era's most "powerful/influential" figures [kennedy (known as "The Haircut"), j. edgar hoover, howard hughes] to low-level pawns ruthlessly manipulated by crooked cops, mafiosa and the like is as addictive as the uncut heroin that, in ellroy's world, finances world-stage events including the bay of pigs, the kennedy assasination, etc.<br /><br />think of _american tabloid_ as something like a fictional _people's history_ of 1959-1963, gore vidal on a really bad acid trip, or just what it claims to be: a tabloid rendering of critical moments in US history.<br /><br />the novel is definitely a "grower," with plot intrigue overshadowing ellroy's painfully hardboiled style only after several hundred pages that establish a web of CIA/FBI/Justice Department/Mafia/Cuban alliances and counter-alliances, enabling a deeply impressive scope and ambition for _american tabloid_. regardless, one must be prepared to wade through over 500 pages of countless plot twists and one-sentence, three-word paragraphs to realize the novel's full payoff. while the tradeoff was ultimately worth it for me, i suspect others might feel differently.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7699532725595794736" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7878850029192327749">(this was a diversion, something to transport)<br/><br/>Much of the GR community shares a united front on American Tabloid, comparing it to meth or serial lines of blow, Ellroy is credited with thousands of pages of plot stripped down to slide into a mere 600 page volume. There is a measure of truth in said consensus. Well some of the metaphors do work. It does often appear that an acetylene torch is applied to the reader's soul. Events do come tumbling into focus and then disappear in the span of a few p</span>
  <span id="freeText7878850029192327749" style="display:none">(this was a diversion, something to transport)<br /><br />Much of the GR community shares a united front on American Tabloid, comparing it to meth or serial lines of blow, Ellroy is credited with thousands of pages of plot stripped down to slide into a mere 600 page volume. There is a measure of truth in said consensus. Well some of the metaphors do work. It does often appear that an acetylene torch is applied to the reader's soul. Events do come tumbling into focus and then disappear in the span of a few pages. The historical significance trails afterward like a sonic boom. The novel's chief created characters ( as opposed to the historical personages that the author stacks to the rafters) all occupy the opaque underworld of the FBI and the Syndicates. The Mob and communist inspire night terrors. Affairs branch outward from there. No one can afford loyalty, we understand. A subplot involving the daughters of the G-men being friends is but a plot device, quickly discarded to no real effect. Many of the characters decide that they don’t hate sufficiently and question matters. I’m guessing a few readers came to the same conclusion. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7878850029192327749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_201095857" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201095857" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/88295-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1232290261p2/88295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201095857?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/88295-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/88295-michael?shelf=bullshit">bullshit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/88295-michael?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201095857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9931326501948188904">This is the first Ellroy I've read, and it will likely be the last. Mostly because I find it impossible to take this seriously.<br/><br/>I don't doubt for a minute his portrayal of mobsters and G-men and teamsters run amok in the fifties and sixties; I'm sure they were just as violent and hellbent on mayhem as they're depicted here. His gloss on the Bay of Pigs jibes, too. There is <i>one</i> neat bit of business following a character's slow arc from soft-skinned do-gooder alcoholic into revenge-driven killer. B</span>
  <span id="freeText9931326501948188904" style="display:none">This is the first Ellroy I've read, and it will likely be the last. Mostly because I find it impossible to take this seriously.<br /><br />I don't doubt for a minute his portrayal of mobsters and G-men and teamsters run amok in the fifties and sixties; I'm sure they were just as violent and hellbent on mayhem as they're depicted here. His gloss on the Bay of Pigs jibes, too. There is <i>one</i> neat bit of business following a character's slow arc from soft-skinned do-gooder alcoholic into revenge-driven killer. But that character's evolution isn't near interesting enough.<br /><br />More the problem is the prose, which is written in a kind of Ellroy clipart style. Every time someone in this book gets punched, that person spits out a tooth. Or swallows a tooth. Or (memorably) spits out a bit of bridgework. If some guys are going to be taken out by shooting, and there is <i>anything</i> combustible nearby, the bodies, the car, the trees, the prose will all be set afire. Just about every time. If someone gets cut, it is always "to the bone." Nearly six hundred pages of this tedious, repetitive twaddle. The ridiculous "hard-boiled" voice keeps the characters from ever gaining more than two-dimensions and always at arm's remove. <br /><br />People love Ellroy, I get that. A friend recommended this book. But not this reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9931326501948188904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1409101919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer504272895945259248">B's got a crush on Pete Bondurant from ever since way back when he first read <i>The Cold Six Thousand</i> but Kemper Boyd's my guy - gets me every time with his <i>classic compartmentalization</i> (nobody likes Ward, but I have a little bit of a soft spot for him). I put off finishing this for as long as I could because I didn't want it to end because it's totally brilliant and because Ellroy does the passage of time so well that even though it'd been two days and I was halfway through it felt like I'd lived</span>
  <span id="freeText504272895945259248" style="display:none">B's got a crush on Pete Bondurant from ever since way back when he first read <i>The Cold Six Thousand</i> but Kemper Boyd's my guy - gets me every time with his <i>classic compartmentalization</i> (nobody likes Ward, but I have a little bit of a soft spot for him). I put off finishing this for as long as I could because I didn't want it to end because it's totally brilliant and because Ellroy does the passage of time so well that even though it'd been two days and I was halfway through it felt like I'd lived the same weeks the characters had , but sometimes you've got to just get right in there &amp; figure out who's going to get shot, so here we are. No complaints. </span>
  <a data-text-id="504272895945259248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1135508787" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1135508787" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1135508787?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679403913" href="/book/show/541008.American_Tabloid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=crime-and-spy-shit">crime-and-spy-shit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1135508787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17614227520187520629">I just posted this but I was listening to the Fugs and this song should be the fucking theme to this book:<br/><br/><i>Who can kill a general in his bed?<br/>Overthrow dictators if they're Red?<br/>Fucking A-man!<br/>CIA man!<br/><br/>Who can buy a government so cheap?<br/>Change a cabinet without a squeak?<br/>Fucking A-man!<br/>CIA man!<br/><br/>Who can train guerrillas by the dozens?<br/>Send them out to kill their untrained cousins?<br/>Fucking A-man!<br/>CIA man!<br/><br/>Who can get a budget that's so great?<br/>Who will be the 51st state?<br/>Who has got the secret-est service?<br/>One tha</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17614227520187520629" style="display:none">I just posted this but I was listening to the Fugs and this song should be the fucking theme to this book:<br /><br /><i>Who can kill a general in his bed?<br />Overthrow dictators if they're Red?<br />Fucking A-man!<br />CIA man!<br /><br />Who can buy a government so cheap?<br />Change a cabinet without a squeak?<br />Fucking A-man!<br />CIA man!<br /><br />Who can train guerrillas by the dozens?<br />Send them out to kill their untrained cousins?<br />Fucking A-man!<br />CIA man!<br /><br />Who can get a budget that's so great?<br />Who will be the 51st state?<br />Who has got the secret-est service?<br />One that makes the other service nervous?<br />Fucking A-man!<br />CIA man!<br /><br />Who can take the sugar from a sack<br />Pour in LSD and put it back?<br />Fucking A-man!<br />CIA man!<br /><br />Who can mine the harbors of Nicaragua?<br />Outhit all the hitmen of Chicag-ua?<br />Fucking A-man!<br />CIA man!<br /><br />Who can be so overtly covert?<br />Sometimes ever covertly overt?<br />Fucking A-man!<br />CIA man!</i><br /><br />Books like <i>The Big Nowhere</i>, <i>L.A. Confidential</i> and <i>White Jazz</i> revealed Ellroy's appetite for writing about labyrinthine criminal conspiracies so while it was certainly a bold step it was not surprising for him to tackle one of the absolute all-time motherfuckers of conspiracies--(if it exists; I'm not even gonna fucking go near that can of worms but I will say that this book is often disturbingly plausible and thoroughly thought through, as are all of this man's books)--the clandestine coordination of dark forces of the American 20th century resulting in the 1963 public murder of President John Kennedy in Dallas. <i>American Tabloid</i> tells the story of three men as they become more and more involved in a nightmarish universe of sketchy CIA men, bloodthirsty mobsters, psychotic Cuban exiles and powerful historical forces in the form of Howard Hughes, Joe, Jack and Bobby Kennedy and J. Edgar Hoover, who comes off like Darth fucking Vader in this book.<br /><br />The three-guy POV marks a return to the grandiose scope of <i>L.A. Confidential</i> after the relatively-disappointing-for-me downscaling in <i>White Jazz</i>. A story this size demands more than one "protagonist" (I use the term loosely, as these are guys are Ellroyian wunderkind in the sense that they embody every thing the author likes to make his characters: amoral, complex, damaged, obsessive, violent, etc.) and Ellroy delivers with three of his best characters yet. We have Pete Bondurant, who attentive readers will remember from <i>White Jazz</i> (and maybe before that too? I don't remember.) Pete isn't even really a cop anymore; he left the force after beating some dude to death, so now he works for Howard Hughes and does odd jobs for mob figures like Jimmy Hoffa (this usually means murdering someone in cold blood, which Pete seems to do every chapter.) Pete's of the Buzz Meeks-Bud White-Dave Klein pedigree, but a straight up bad guy--although not without his complexities and surprises for the reader.<br /><br />Next up is Kemper Boyd. Man, lots of conflicting feelings for this guy--most of them bad. He's obsessed with money, the glorious image and ascent of the Kennedy family, and generally being the personification of the sneaky-sketchy CIA/FBI spook stereotype from the 50s and 60s. The weird thing about Kemper is that even though he's not quite as violent as Pete he grossed me out even further with his obsessive idea of "compartmentalization" and pledging loyalty to like four or five different, often competing agencies/causes/people while keeping it all a secret. I think he's a brilliant embodiment of the ugly, dark of America of this time period: the part obsessed with money, power and secrets and the thrills people get from them--constantly fucking with other people's shit and switching sides on a whim. So yeah...not a huge fan of Kemper Boyd. Being an Ellroy character and not being all one thing or the other, though, he has some redeemable qualities. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[His somewhat genuine interest and half-assed dedication to civil rights issues was kinda nice, especially when he shot that church-bombing hick in the face...also I felt bad during the part where Ward kills him and his last thought is of Jack. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Kemper was kind of a new thing for Ellroy, although he does have some similarities to guys like Jack Vincennes and dare I say Dudley Smith?<br /><br />Last is Ward Littell, who was a Jesuit seminarian and now works as an FBI agent. On the whole I liked Ward. He's constantly torn between knowing the right thing to do and having the strength to do it, which is one of the MOST human things ever. He also has a very realistically drawn alcohol problem. He's probably the most normal, relatable character in this entire fucking book but this being Ellroy's world you gotta take it with a grain of salt. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I mean...dude ends up fucking murdering the guy that was probably his closest friend and then orchestrates the fucking Kennedy assassination. Talk about character development! I definitely "liked" Ward at the beginning in the sense that you gravitate towards the least dangerous-looking person in a threatening environment...not particularly now. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> One thing I did notice is although Littell presumably has a shitload of religious/spiritual baggage Ellroy didn't seem interested in brutally exploiting it like he does with all of the skeletons in his characters' closets. Come to think of it, he doesn't really touch on religion in ANY of his books...could it be that this is the literal one thing on Earth that is taboo for Ellroy? Littell is very much in the Mal Considine-Ed Exley gene pool but he definitely has his surprises.<br /><br />So already we have a great cast of characters historical and fictional to run around setting up all these interconnected/competing plots and watching the results, which as we all know are FUCKED up. This goes back to Ellroy's significant increase in scope with this book. We know that there's a bridge between the world of the L.A. Quartet and this one because characters from that series show up in this one and <i>Hush-Hush</i> is still very much a thing (as you may have guessed from the title), but now we're getting to directly see the perpetration of huge historical crimes like the Kennedy assassination whereas real-life stuff like the Sleepy Lagoon murder from <i>Big Nowhere</i> was just kind of setting and background plot. I don't want to play up the assassination part too much, though; that's dealt with at the very end of the novel, leaving like 600 pages of horrible, horrible criminal behavior of every make and model. This is definitely the darkest, grimmest Ellroy novel I've read as of yet. Illegal wiretapping, chainsaw dismemberment, scalp-hunting boat raids into Cuba, etc. abound and are rendered even more disturbing by Ellroy's utterly matter-of-fact and clipped delivery. Which brings me to style!<br /><br />No big changes in Ellroy's prose here. To be honest, it felt more akin to <i>L.A. Confidential</i> than <i>White Jazz</i>, which was almost TOO brutal and staccato at times. The writing here still uses short, jagged phrasing and blocky rhythms but it's smoother, more refined. Okay, THAT is what I mean when I say that Ellroy's prose sounds like music. That sentence totally sounds like I'm describing a guitar player or something! The man is a world-class stylist, able to reach even the most artless peons like myself. I really love the lick where he just goes, I don't have the book but it'd be something like: <br /><br />"Stoic Lenny: 'I didn't hear anything. <br />Shaky Lenny: 'Okay, maybe I heard something.' <br />Broken Lenny: 'I'll fucking tell you everything!'" <br /><br />Listen...one thing you will notice if you read more than like three or four reviews of Ellroy books is this uncontrollable urge by the reviewer to imitate the author's style. I think I held off long enough, and my results were poor enough to ensure I never do it again. I apologize to everyone who read it, but it had to be done.<br /><br />Another masterpiece from Ellroy. The dude just writes really, really great fucking books. The only caveat I have, which is par for the course with this author, is that if you're really bothered by constant racial and sexual invective, bloody and gruesome violence and a story in which somehow EVERYONE comes out looking bad, even American sacred cows like John F. and Robert Kennedy you should avoid this like the plague. Ellroy virgins are encouraged to start with earlier stuff but fuck it, if you're feeling bold give this one a shot. To the already converted, this is a total no-brainer. It only makes me more excited to continue this trilogy with <i>The Cold Six Thousand</i>. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Although I'm not sure how Ellroy's gonna propel the story without a brilliant shit-shaker-upper like Kemper Boyd. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="17614227520187520629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_863688480" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/863688480" />
    <a title="Curtis Retherford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27534246-curtis-retherford"><img alt="Curtis Retherford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389336564p2/27534246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/863688480?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Curtis Retherford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Curtis Retherford" href="/user/show/27534246-curtis-retherford">Curtis Retherford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27534246-curtis-retherford?shelf=book-club-books">book-club-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer863688480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer172308305526550556">Reprehensible and inept.<br/><br/>The characters are almost entirely identical: They talk the same, they think the same, they all stumble through the plot in the same way: numb, cocksure, and without any discernible motivation for any of their actions. This book lacks even the cookie-cutter archetypes of a standard noir, which it so clearly wants to be. Instead, each character simply goes from crime to crime with no particular purpose. They are devoid of morals, but Ellroy has failed to give anything inte</span>
  <span id="freeText172308305526550556" style="display:none">Reprehensible and inept.<br /><br />The characters are almost entirely identical: They talk the same, they think the same, they all stumble through the plot in the same way: numb, cocksure, and without any discernible motivation for any of their actions. This book lacks even the cookie-cutter archetypes of a standard noir, which it so clearly wants to be. Instead, each character simply goes from crime to crime with no particular purpose. They are devoid of morals, but Ellroy has failed to give anything interesting to make up for that. They seem to care a little about money, but not particularly. Why do things happen? Who the fuck knows. Things happen, and the characters consistently pat themselves on the back for their actions, but it's all meaningless. Near the end of the book, there is a clumsy attempt at giving each of the main characters a motivation, but by that point it is so late, and so much has happened, that it no longer matters.<br /><br />The plot is essentially a shaggy dog story. Alliances shift and twist, but not towards any particular goal. Characters frequently have to repeat to themselves and each other exactly what is happening, because the plot changes so often. Characters will instantly offer up indiscretions to people they have just met for the sole purpose of either re-explaining the current plot or to give another character some leverage, to force yet another shift in allegiances and power. Every plot point is cartoonish, an over-the-top attempt at shocking the reader via sex (typically homosexual sex) or violence. It quickly gets boring.<br /><br />I'll give Ellroy the benefit of the doubt and assume that the narrator is not Ellroy, but simply a character whom we never meet. In that case, the narrator is a racist, homophobic, fumbling writer. Although he tries to write in a Chandler-esque hard-boiled noir style, with short, choppy sentences, he isn't an adept enough writer to pull it off. Sentences end up limp and vague, with ambiguous pronouns and misplaced modifiers littered throughout. The only reason this doesn't completely stall any forward momentum is that Ellroy tells, rather than shows. He describes little actual action, and instead has the characters tell and retell each other what has happened, or is happening.<br /><br />However, even assuming the narrator is not Ellroy, there are two problems that fall purely in Ellroy's lap: the dialogue and the document inserts. Every character in this book speaks in the exact same way. The dialogue of Pete Bondurant, a gigantic French-Canadian tough, and the dialogue of Kemper Boyd, a highly educated FBI agent from Tennessee, are both interchangeable. Ellroy is obsessed with regional dialects (characters often remark on the accents or dialects of other characters), and yet Ellroy has a tin ear for human speech. The only time Ellroy changes any dialogue at all is for his Jewish characters, who throw in an absurd number of Yiddish terms into their conversations. Then, there are the document inserts: They read like more Ellroy, rather than any sort of official document.<br /><br />This isn't a novel, it's a conspiracy theory, and as such it shares the problems of most conspiracy theories. It is sprawling, tying in every conceivable person in the area with no rhyme or reason. Any famous person from the late 50s and early 60s shows up here. Ellroy's favorite tactic is to name-drop a celebrity and then casually mention what sexual deviancy they were guilty of. Ellroy is obsessed with certain details, such as the makes and models of firearms, but knows nothing about the other elements, such as what happens when someone gets shot, or when a toaster is thrown into a sink, et cetera. The few action scenes in the book are therefore rushed through, with ludicrous all caps lines such as "The Molotov hit the pavement AND DID NOT SHATTER."<br /><br />The book also shares the biggest problem of most conspiracy theories: a basic lack of understanding of how humans behave. It treats each person as essentially identical, a person to put in place, as if the plot were a chess puzzle, and you merely need to worry about getting each piece in the right location at the right time, and it doesn't matter how or why they got there.<br /><br />This book is garbage.</span>
  <a data-text-id="172308305526550556" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_75144663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75144663" />
    <a title="Nick Black" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/655723-nick-black"><img alt="Nick Black" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315018121p2/655723.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75144663?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Black" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Black" href="/user/show/655723-nick-black">Nick Black</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/655723-nick-black?shelf=likely-reread">likely-reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75144663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11416781812148321534">It had some really memorable lines, but overall I dug <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4191.The_Cold_Six_Thousand' title='The Cold Six Thousand by James Ellroy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Cold Six Thousand</a> more. We'll see how <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6094181.Blood_s_A_Rover' title='Blood's A Rover by James Ellroy' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blood's a Rover</a> goes, but overall I'm thinking James Ellroy is something I'd enjoyed much more eight years ago. What's the <i>point</i>, Pete/Boyd/Wayne/Littell? For such multitalented, resourceful and industrious individuals, they sure seem to lack any meaningful <i>weltanschauung</i> of their own, content to play the part of vessels. Not a stride is broken upon the death of XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX late in this firs</span>
  <span id="freeText11416781812148321534" style="display:none">It had some really memorable lines, but overall I dug <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4191.The_Cold_Six_Thousand" title="The Cold Six Thousand by James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Cold Six Thousand</a> more. We'll see how <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6094181.Blood_s_A_Rover" title="Blood's A Rover by James Ellroy" rel="nofollow noopener">Blood's a Rover</a> goes, but overall I'm thinking James Ellroy is something I'd enjoyed much more eight years ago. What's the <i>point</i>, Pete/Boyd/Wayne/Littell? For such multitalented, resourceful and industrious individuals, they sure seem to lack any meaningful <i>weltanschauung</i> of their own, content to play the part of vessels. Not a stride is broken upon the death of XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX late in this first book, knowing another untermensch will rise up from Ellroy's underworld miasma.<br /><br />And the formulaic, almost algorithmic nature! What percentage of (super-short, so you feel deliciously smart as you're chugging through them) chapters could be plotted in one line:<br /><br />{ Major character X momentary ally X minor character X ( brace | kill | twist!) }?<br /><br />Sure, the welling-up and breaking-down Reverse-Everyman is one of Ellroy's major points, but if you're going to talk about meanings and themes you must move into the realm of Serious Literature, which Mr. Ellroy decidedly is <i>not</i>.<br /><br />James Edgar Hoover as written by Ellroy is one of the most spectacular characters I've ever seen, though he benefits tremendously from foiling; one gets the feeling conversations with JEH, for all their trenchant brevity, consumed a majority of Ellroy's writing time (or were done in single drafts, either way -- as unforgettably recorded by Goodie Mob, "ain't no in-between you either off or on"). The Kennedys could have been more vividly painted had they been seen only through radio and television broadcasts; they're devices and nothing more. Pete has limitless promise, and could play the role of a Hagbard Celine, but he's confused more than anything. Confusion, though, is the vantage point least amenable to decisive action, the only action in which Pete engages. My engineer-self recoils in horror from what could otherwise be a sympathetic character.<br /><br />In the interests of full disclosure, I found myself regularly bewildered, scandalized, and finally dismissively annoyed by Ellroy's Deep South, populated exclusively by Klansmen, Cubans and Negroes (thankfully, he restricted himself mainly to dread Mississippi, indeed a blighted place). I've lived in Atlanta all my years, and in that time have seen Klan influence, or indeed the overt racial tension visible in parts of New York, Boston, Detroit and LA, only in the portrayals of fiction, prime time, and movies (and David Duke, but Louisiana is its own hellhole, for which the southeast accepts no responsibility. Blame Texas; blame France). I've no doubt things improved immeasurably since '70, especially in the rural areas, but really all any unfortunate inhabitant of the rural South ought be thinking about is How are we Getting to Atlanta?, When the Hell are we Leaving This Godforsaken Place?, Why Aren't we Departing Now?, and Where is the Nearest Chik-fil-a?<br /><br />----<br />Amazon 2009-10-20. Fiction must return to my life by the end of the year, and y'all rave on about this fellow like no one else.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11416781812148321534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_99344383" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99344383" />
    <a title="Gus Sanchez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1047204-gus-sanchez"><img alt="Gus Sanchez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520308903p2/1047204.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gus Sanchez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gus Sanchez" href="/user/show/1047204-gus-sanchez">Gus Sanchez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99344383" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17606311185087411253">Wow.<br/><br/>Yeah. Wow. <br/><br/>This was my first James Ellroy novel, and he did not disappoint. On the contrary, I developed a major hard-on for his hard-assed prose, and his dark, morally ambiguous characters - gotta say Pete Bondurant is now one of my favorite fictional characters ever. <br/><br/>I won't bore you with the details or the plot behind "American Tabloid", the first in a trilogy of works sketching out the nefarious doings of those in power, but if you love fiction that's both hard-edged and replete with his</span>
  <span id="freeText17606311185087411253" style="display:none">Wow.<br /><br />Yeah. Wow. <br /><br />This was my first James Ellroy novel, and he did not disappoint. On the contrary, I developed a major hard-on for his hard-assed prose, and his dark, morally ambiguous characters - gotta say Pete Bondurant is now one of my favorite fictional characters ever. <br /><br />I won't bore you with the details or the plot behind "American Tabloid", the first in a trilogy of works sketching out the nefarious doings of those in power, but if you love fiction that's both hard-edged and replete with historical heavyweights - the Kennedys, J. Edgar Hoover, Howard Hughes, Sam Giancana, just to name a few - this novel was written for you. It does drag on a bit, and your patience and attention is greatly needed, but Ellroy pays off, in spades. <br /><br />I loved this book so much I immediately began reading Ellroy's second part to this trilogy, "The Cold Six Thousand," just so I can see what kind of hinky shit Pete Bondurant and Ward Littell have gotten themselves into. <br /><br />Most highly recommended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17606311185087411253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2617375617" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2617375617" />
    <a title="Tom Mooney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22601722-tom-mooney"><img alt="Tom Mooney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539085918p2/22601722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2617375617?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Mooney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Mooney" href="/user/show/22601722-tom-mooney">Tom Mooney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2617375617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17006425375035601">No one but Ellroy could pull this off. This is a takedown of the Kennedys. Of Jack and Bobby, and Joe too. An expose of the lurid collusion between the mob, the CIA, the FBI, the teamsters and the government. It's 10 books of plot rammed into 600 frantic, bloody, filthy pages.<br/><br/>It's genius. But now I'm worn out and tired. I feel like I got a shakedown from Pete Bondurant. Until next time, Mr Ellroy.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2617375617">
  <td colspan="2">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_190671643" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190671643" />
    <a title="Colin McKay Miller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/739464-colin-mckay-miller"><img alt="Colin McKay Miller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442509875p2/739464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/190671643?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Colin McKay Miller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Colin McKay Miller" href="/user/show/739464-colin-mckay-miller">Colin McKay Miller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who can stand Ellroy's bent</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/739464-colin-mckay-miller?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer190671643" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15888565464771041608">James Ellroy’s <i>American Tabloid</i> may not be as good as <i>The Black Dahlia</i>, but it’s still an excellent, historically-based crime novel.<br/><br/>James Ellroy isn’t for everyone. It isn’t just his staccato neo-noir voice or his violent stories. He often writes characters that, at the very least, display racist and misogynistic tendencies. Ellroy himself has been accused of these very things and has responded in, well, <i>Ellroy-like</i> fashion (pretty much him insulting and cussing at people without really addressi</span>
  <span id="freeText15888565464771041608" style="display:none">James Ellroy’s <i>American Tabloid</i> may not be as good as <i>The Black Dahlia</i>, but it’s still an excellent, historically-based crime novel.<br /><br />James Ellroy isn’t for everyone. It isn’t just his staccato neo-noir voice or his violent stories. He often writes characters that, at the very least, display racist and misogynistic tendencies. Ellroy himself has been accused of these very things and has responded in, well, <i>Ellroy-like</i> fashion (pretty much him insulting and cussing at people without really addressing the issue). Make no mistake, Ellroy <i>is</i> messed up. The murder of his mother in his youth has shaped both his work and his taste in women; so again, I don’t fault anyone who can’t connect with this type of material.<br /><br />If you can though, <i>American Tabloid</i> is rich and deep. The novel covers the historical U.S. events of November 1958 to November 1963 and essentially writes in three main fictional characters to shape the key events during that time. They include: former Los Angeles deputy turned private eye/crime associate Pete Bondurant; southern FBI agent Kemper Boyd; and FBI agent (and friend/former partner of Kemper Boyd) Ward Littell. All three work for and against various historical figures—including Jimmy Hoffa, J. Edgar Hoover, John and Robert Kennedy, Howard Hughes—and with and against each other, depending on what fits their needs. Like other Ellroy works, these three men go through changes in power and personality, and the appeal is seeing where they’ll end up by the novel’s end. Unlike <i>The Black Dahlia</i>—which I think benefits from stronger relationships between fewer characters—<i>American Tabloid</i> ties in political agendas of large organizations including the mafia, the FBI, the CIA, Fidel Castro’s communist regime, and the White House itself. Conspiracies run amok, and Ellroy crams so much plot and so many twists into the pages that I doubt even he can keep it straight, but it’s entertaining enough that you roll with the confusion.<br /><br />My favorite character has to be comedian turned informant, Lenny Sands. Unlike the main characters that seem to be written as a tad too resilient (even if they do get broken down from time to time), Lenny Sands has secrets and manipulations from which he can’t escape. He’s probably top 10 character material for me if not higher. However, as consistently good as <i>American Tabloid</i> is, sometimes Ellroy gets stuck in his little loops and ruts. More than once, there were pages full of nothing but repetitive two-line paragraphs, several people get slapped and swallow teeth like it’s no big thing, and in the end, Ellroy writes a key plot point that doesn’t make sense. Remember, this is a book where characters and allegiances shift constantly, so it takes Ellroy being way far off base for me to call him out on it. Despite these flaws, American Tabloid holds up well over its near-600 pages, and much like his L.A. quartet series, I plan on continuing on with this Underworld USA trilogy. Three stars and reaching higher. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15888565464771041608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7281955968077245256">James Ellroy was born in Los Angeles in 1948. His L.A. Quartet novels—<i>The Black Dahlia</i>, <i>The Big Nowhere</i>, <i>L.A. Confidential</i>, and <i>White Jazz</i>—were international best sellers. His novel <i>American Tabloid</i> was <i>Time</i> magazine’s Best Book (fiction) of 1995; his memoir, <i>My Dark Places</i>, was a <i>Time</i> Best Book of the Year and a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book for 1996. His novel <i>The Cold Six Thousand</i> was a <i>New York </i></span>
  <span id="freeText7281955968077245256" style="display:none">James Ellroy was born in Los Angeles in 1948. His L.A. Quartet novels—<i>The Black Dahlia</i>, <i>The Big Nowhere</i>, <i>L.A. Confidential</i>, and <i>White Jazz</i>—were international best sellers. His novel <i>American Tabloid</i> was <i>Time</i> magazine’s Best Book (fiction) of 1995; his memoir, <i>My Dark Places</i>, was a <i>Time</i> Best Book of the Year and a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book for 1996. His novel <i>The Cold Six Thousand</i> was a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book and a <i>Los Angeles Times</i> Best Book for 2001. Ellroy lives in Los Angeles.<br /><br />Ellroy is known for a "telegraphic" writing style, which omits words other writers would consider necessary, and often features sentence fragments. His books are noted for their dark humor and depiction of American authoritarianism. Other hallmarks of his work include dense plotting and a relentlessly pessimistic worldview. Ellroy has been called the "Demon Dog of American crime fiction."<br /><br />See also <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0255278/">http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0255278/</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7281955968077245256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

Their job: to clean up the JFK hit’s loose ends and in<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7609268615147001947\" style=\"display:none\">In this savagely audacious novel, James Ellroy plants a pipe bomb under the America in the 1960s, lights the fuse, and watches the shrapnel fly. On November 22, 1963 three men converge in Dallas. Their job: to clean up the JFK hit’s loose ends and inconvenient witnesses. They are Wayne Tedrow, Jr., a Las Vegas cop with family ties to the lunatic right; Ward J. Littell, a defrocked FBI man turned underworld mouthpiece; and Pete Bondurant, a dope-runner and hit-man who serves as the mob’s emissary to the anti-Castro underground.It goes bad from there. For the next five years these night-riders run a whirlwind of plots and counter-plots: Howard Hughes’s takeover of Vegas, J. Edgar Hoover’s war against the civil rights movement, the heroin trade in Vietnam, and the murders of Martin Luther King, Jr. and Bobby Kennedy. Wilder than L. A.       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/35975-american-tabloid">Quotes from American Tabloid</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;America was never innocent. We popped our cherry on the boat over and looked back with no regrets. You can't ascribe our fall from grace to any single event or set of circumstances. You can't lose what you lacked at conception.<br />Mass-market nostalgia gets you hopped up for a past that never existed. Hagiography sanctifies shuck-and-jive politicians and reinvents their expedient gestures as moments of great moral weight. Our continuing narrative line is blurred past truth and hindsight. Only a reckless verisimilitude can set that line straight.<br />The real Trinity of Camelot was Look Good, Kick Ass, Get Laid. Jack Kennedy was the mythological front man for a particularly juicy slice of our history. He called a slick line and wore a world-class haircut. He was Bill Clinton minus pervasive media scrutiny and a few rolls of flab.<br />Jack got whacked at the optimum moment to assure his sainthood. Lies continue to swirl around his eternal flame. It's time to dislodge his urn and cast light on a few men who attended his ascent and facilitated his fall.<br />They were rouge cops and shakedown artist. They were wiretappers and soldiers of fortune and faggot lounge entertainers. Had one second of their lives deviated off course, American History would not exist as we know it.<br />It's time to demythologize an era and build a new myth from the gutter to the stars. It's time to embrace bad men and the price they paid to secretly define there time.<br />Here's to them.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/400991-america-was-never-innocent-we-popped-our-cherry-on-the">29 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Jimmy Hoffa said, “I know how Jesus must have felt. The fucking pharaohs rose to power on his coattails like the fucking Kennedy brothers are rising on mine.”<br /><br />Heshie Ryskind said, “Get your history straight. It was Julius Caesar that did Jesus in.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/688722-jimmy-hoffa-said-i-know-how-jesus-must-have-felt">7 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/35975-american-tabloid">More quotes&hellip;</a>
